3
Understand rational and legal dimensions
See why the argument uses both rational ('aql) and legal (sam') reasoning. The permissibility is first established rationally, then affirmed by revelation.
4
Examine exceptions and conditions
The passage states that major sins are forgiven after repentance, but can also be forgiven without repentance at God's will. Disbelief, however, is not forgiven according to revelation.

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on forgiveness of sins other than disbelief. Definition of Forgiveness and the Wisdom Behind Forgiving Sins but Not Disbelief
Author defines forgiveness and explains the wisdom of its limited scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of forgiveness in Islamic theology and the reasons why sins are forgiven while disbelief is not.

Core Idea
Forgiveness means waiving punishment after accountability, not erasing sin without consequence.

This passage from the author clarifies what divine forgiveness and pardon entail—not mere overlooking but a deliberate act after accountability. It then delves into the wisdom behind why sins against Allah are forgiven while disbelief remains unpardonable. The distinction rests on the nature of disbelief as a permanent conviction versus sins as momentary lapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its structure. Then read the English translation. Note that the author first defines forgiveness, then gives reasons for the difference between sin and disbelief.
2
Understand the definition of forgiveness
Focus on the definition: forgiveness (ghufran) and pardon (afw) mean leaving the punishment of the sinner and covering the sin after taking account. Recognise that this implies a two-step process: accountability first, then forgiveness.
The term 'after accountability' is crucial—forgiveness is not a simple overlooking.
3
Identify the reasons for forgiving sins but not disbelief
The passage gives three reasons for this distinction. List them: (1) sins are accompanied by fear and hope, disbelief is not; (2) sins are momentary, disbelief is a permanent belief; (3) disbelief's sanctity cannot be removed, so its punishment cannot be removed.
These reasons are not exhaustive but highlight key differences in nature.
4
Examine the key terms
Notice the terms 'غفران' (forgiveness) and 'عفو' (pardon). The definition uses both, suggesting they are closely related but not identical. Reflect on the nuance.
The author uses two terms to cover the idea of leaving punishment and concealment.
5
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider the larger lesson: God's forgiveness is wise and just. It is not arbitrary. The reasons given show that the nature of the sin or disbelief determines its possibility of forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بغفرانها والعفو عنها: ترك عقوبة صاحبها والستر عليه بعد المؤاخذة. والحكمة في غفران المعاصي دون الكفر: أنها لا تنفك عن خوف عقاب ورجاء عفو ورحمة وغير ذلك، بخلاف الكفر، ولأنها لوقت الهوى والشهوة فقط، بخلاف الكفر؛ فإنه مذهب يعتقد للأبد، وحرمته لا تحتمل الارتفاع أصلا، فكذلك عقوبته، بخلاف المعصية.
And what is meant by forgiveness and pardon regarding them: is leaving the punishment of its owner and concealing [the sin] after accountability. And the wisdom in forgiving sins without disbelief: is that they are not free from fear of punishment and hope of forgiveness and mercy and other than that, unlike disbelief; and because they are only for the time of desire and passion, unlike disbelief; for it is a belief that is held forever, and its sacredness does not admit of removal at all, so likewise its punishment, unlike sin.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and a rationale. First, it defines forgiveness (ghufran) and pardon (afw) as leaving the punishment of the sinner and covering the sin after taking account. This implies that forgiveness does not mean erasing the sin without accountability, but rather after accountability the punishment is dropped and the sin is concealed. Then, it explains the wisdom behind why sins (ma'asi) are forgiven but disbelief (kufr) is not. The reasons given are: (1) sins are accompanied by psychological states like fear of punishment and hope of forgiveness and mercy, unlike disbelief which lacks these; (2) sins are committed due to momentary desire and passion, while disbelief is a permanent belief (madhhab) that one holds forever; and (3) the sanctity of disbelief is such that it cannot be removed at all, so its punishment is likewise eternal, unlike sins which are temporary and can be forgiven.

Correction: The passage clarifies that forgiveness involves leaving punishment and concealing after accountability, indicating a two-step process.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Fate of the Unrepentant Major Sinner
Defines the issue of the unrepentant major sinner and states the position of Ahl al-Haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of Ahl al-Haqq regarding the fate of a Muslim who dies unrepentant from a major sin.

Core Idea
The unrepentant sinner's fate is in Allah's will, but not eternal.

This lesson addresses a critical question in Islamic theology: what happens to a Muslim who dies without repenting from major sins? The passage from al-Sanusi explains the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) — neither unconditional forgiveness nor eternal punishment, but delegation to Allah's will with eventual salvation. This nuanced view balances God's justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the issue
Read the passage orientation and the first sentence to grasp what theological question is being discussed.
The issue is known by several names: threat against sinners, punishment of the disobedient, cessation of punishment.
2
Learn the precise definition
Identify the three conditions in the definition: a believer commits a major sin (not expelling from faith), without considering it permissible, and dies without repentance.
This is the 'ḍābiṭ' — the exact scope of the issue.
3
Absorb the position of Ahl al-Haqq
Notice that they do not decide between forgiveness or punishment definitively; it is under Allah's will. However, if punishment occurs, it is temporary — the person will not abide eternally in Hell.
The reasoning: sins are not made permissible by guaranteeing forgiveness, and punishment is not guaranteed because Allah may forgive.
4
Examine the scriptural evidence
Read the evidence from Quran and hadith: the verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' and the hadith 'Whoever says la ilaha illa Allah will enter Paradise.' Understand how these support the position that believers will ultimately enter Paradise.
The entrance is either after purification in the Fire or directly without it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ يَمُتْ ولم يَتُبْ مِنْ ذَنْبِهِ فأمرُهُ مُفَوَّض لِرَبِّهِ. هذه المسألةُ ترجمها بعضُهم بمسألة: وعيد الفُسَّاق ، وتَرجمها بعضُهم بمسألة: عقوبة العصاة، وبَعضُهم ترجمها بمسألة : انقطاع عذاب أهل الكبائر . وضابطها : أنْ يَرتكب المؤمنُ كبيرةً غيرَ مُكفِّرة، بلا استحلال، ويموت بلا توبة. فَأَمْرُهُ مُفَوَّضٌ لِرَبِّهِ. فذهب أهل الحقِّ إلى أنَّه لا يُقطع له بعفو ولا عقاب ، بل هو في مشيئة الله ، وعلى تقدير وقوع العِقاب عدلا منه يقطع له بعدم الخُلودِ في النَّار ، كما أشار إليه بقوله الآتي: ثُمَّ الخُلود مُجتنَبْ بل يخرج منها ، وإنَّما لم يُقطع له بالعفو؛ لئلا تكونَ الذُّنوب في حُكم المُباحَة ، ولا بالعقوبة لما سبقَ مِن أنَّه تعالى يجوزُ عليه أنْ يَعْفِر ما عدا الكفر. قسم السمعيات. تمسَّكَ أصحابنا بما عُمْدَتُهُ الآيات والأحاديث الداله على أنَّ المؤمنين يدخلون الجنَّة البتّة ، كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] . وقوله : «مَن قال لا إله إلَّا الله دخل الجنَّة» وليس ذلك قبل دخول النَّار ؛ فتعيَّن أنْ يكونَ بعده ، وهو مسألة انقطاع العذاب، أو بدونه وهو مسألة العفو التام.
Whoever dies and does not repent from his sin, his affair is delegated to his Lord. This issue some have translated as the issue of the threat against the sinners, and some have translated it as the issue of the punishment of the disobedient, and some have translated it as the issue of the cessation of the punishment of the people of major sins. Its definition: that a believer commits a major sin that is not expelling from faith, without considering it permissible, and dies without repentance. So his affair is delegated to his Lord. The people of truth hold that one cannot definitely conclude for him forgiveness nor punishment, rather he is under God's will; and on the assumption that punishment occurs—justly from Him—one can definitely conclude for him that he will not abide eternally in the Fire, as He indicated by His subsequent saying: 'Then eternal abiding is avoided, rather he will come out of it.' And he did not definitely conclude forgiveness for him, lest sins be in the category of permissible things, nor punishment because of what preceded that the Exalted may forgive anything other than disbelief. Section on the sam‘iyyāt. Our companions held fast to what is based on verses and hadiths indicating that the believers will certainly enter Paradise, such as His saying: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [99:7], and His saying: 'Whoever says there is no god but Allah will enter Paradise.' And that is not before entering the Fire; so it is determined that it is after it, which is the issue of cessation of punishment, or without it, which is the issue of complete forgiveness.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage defines the issue of the unrepentant major sinner and states the position of Ahl al-Haqq. It begins by giving the various names for this issue: 'the threat against the sinners', 'the punishment of the disobedient', or 'the cessation of punishment for major sinners'. Then it provides the precise definition (ḍābiṭ): a believer commits a major sin that does not expel from faith (i.e., not kufr), without considering it permissible (istiḥlāl), and dies without repentance. For such a person, the matter is delegated to Allah. The Ahl al-Haqq do not make a definite judgment of forgiveness or punishment; rather, it is in Allah's will. However, if Allah does punish him (justly), then it is certain that he will not be eternally in Hell; rather, he will eventually come out, as indicated by the author's upcoming line 'then eternal abiding is avoided'. The reason they do not definitively say he is forgiven is to prevent sins from being treated as permissible, and they do not definitively say he is punished because Allah may forgive anything except shirk. Then the passage moves to the evidence from the sam‘iyyāt (scriptural matters): our scholars (aṣḥābunā) rely on the Quran and hadith that believers will certainly enter Paradise, such as the verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' and the hadith 'Whoever says there is no god but Allah will enter Paradise.' This entrance is not before entering the Fire (if they are punished), but after, which is the issue of cessation of punishment, or without entering the Fire, which is the issue of complete forgiveness.

Key Terms
الكبيرة
التوبة
المشيئة
الخلود
العفو
الوعيد
السَمْعِيَّات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ahl al-Haqq teach unconditional forgiveness for all Muslims regardless of sins.
Correction: The passage shows that Ahl al-Haqq do not rule out punishment; they leave the matter to Allah's will and affirm temporary punishment if He wills, but deny eternal punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the precise definition (ḍābiṭ) of the issue discussed?
A) A believer commits a major sin that expels from faith, considers it permissible, and dies without repentance.
B) A believer commits a major sin that does not expel from faith, without considering it permissible, and dies without repentance.
C) A believer commits a minor sin without repentance.
D) A believer commits a major sin and repents before death.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Haqq hold that the unrepentant major sinner is subject to Allah's will; if punished, it is temporary, and the believer will ultimately enter Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die tonight without having repented from a major sin, are you more afraid of Allah's punishment or complacent in His mercy? How does this lesson's balanced position challenge your current state?
?
Do you treat your major sins as 'permissible' through excuses or neglect? What would it take for you to sincerely repent and return to Allah before the delegation of your affair is sealed?
?
This lesson teaches that the believer's ultimate destination is Paradise, but the road may include temporary punishment. Are you living a life that invites Allah's complete forgiveness and bypasses that purification, or are you risk-taking eternal bliss for fleeting pleasures?

The Necessity of Punishment for Some Sinners and No Eternal Punishment
States that it is wajib to punish some major sinners but not eternally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah will necessarily punish some major sinners from this ummah, but not eternally, and the conditions for that punishment.

Core Idea
Allah will punish some major sinners, but not all, and never eternally.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key point of Sunni eschatology: while punishment for some major sinners is established by revelation and consensus, it is limited in scope and duration. This teaching balances divine justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic poem and commentary along with the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the structure: the poem states the claim, the commentary expands on it.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two related claims: (1) punishment for some major sinners is wajib (established/necessary), and (2) eternal abiding in Hell for them is avoided (i.e., they will not be eternally punished).
3
Understand the meaning of 'wajib' here
The commentary clarifies that 'wajib' here means 'thābit' (established) and 'wāqi‘' (occurring) based on revealed evidence (sam‘an) and consensus (ijmā‘an). It is not a moral obligation upon Allah but an established reality.
This is a technical usage in kalam.
4
Note the conditions for punishment
The punishment applies to some unspecified sinners who: (a) committed a major sin (action or omission) intentionally, (b) without a valid legal excuse or interpretation, and (c) died without repentance.
'Some' indicates not all major sinners are necessarily punished.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this doctrine balances divine justice (punishment for sin) and mercy (no eternal punishment for believers who die with faith).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبْ كبيرة ثُمَّ الخلودُ مُجْتَنَبْ. وَ وَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضٍ، أي: اعتقاد أَنْ يُعذِّبَ الله تعالى بعضاً عُصاة هذه الأمة ، غير مُعيَّنٍ ارْتَكَبْ كَبِيرَةً، أي: فعلا أو تركا عمدًا من غير تأويل يُعذرُ به شرعا ، ومات بلا توبة. واجب، أي: ثابت وواقع سمعا وإجماعا.
And it is obligatory (that there is) punishment of some who commit a major sin, then eternal abiding is avoided. 'And it is obligatory to punish some'—meaning: the belief that Allah will punish some of the sinners of this ummah, unspecified, who committed a major sin, i.e., an act or omission intentionally without a valid interpretation excused by the Sacred Law, and died without repentance. 'Obligatory' means established and occurring by revealed evidence and consensus.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage states the second point: that it is wājib (thābit) that Allah will punish some of the sinners of this ummah, but they will not be eternally in Hell. It begins with the poem line: 'And it is obligatory to punish some who commit a major sin, then eternal abiding is avoided.' The commentary explains that 'obligatory to punish some' means the belief (i‘tiqād) that Allah will punish some of the sinners of this ummah, not specified individuals, who committed a major sin (action or omission deliberately, without a valid interpretation that would excuse them legally) and died without repentance. The word 'wājib' here means thābit (established) and wāqi‘ (occurring) based on revealed evidence (sam‘an) and consensus (ijmā‘an). This affirms the reality of punishment for some sinners while denying eternal punishment for them.

Misconception: Some may think that 'wajib' here means every major sinner will definitely be punished, or that it is an obligation upon Allah.
Correction: The commentary explicitly says 'بعض' (some) and explains 'wajib' as thābit and wāqi‘, not a moral obligation. Punishment is for some unspecified sinners, not all.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Allah will necessarily punish some major sinners who die unrepentant, but they will not be eternally in Hell.

Introduction to Sam'iyyat: Punishment and Forgiveness for Believers
Introduction to Sam'iyyat with scriptural proof and two possible outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible outcomes for sinful believers in the afterlife based on scriptural evidence as presented by the author.

Core Idea
Every believer will enter Paradise, either after punishment or without it.

The author begins the section on Sam'iyyat, matters known through revelation, by citing verses and hadiths that guarantee Paradise for believers. He then makes a logical deduction: since this entry is not before the Fire, it must be either after punishment (cessation of punishment) or without it (complete forgiveness). This lesson introduces these two possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author states the position, gives evidence, then deduces the two outcomes.
2
Identify the evidence
Find the two scriptural proofs: the Quranic verse and the hadith. Ask how they support the claim that believers enter Paradise.
3
Understand the logical deduction
The author argues that entering Paradise is not before the Fire, so it is after or without. Think about why this matters for the discussion on punishment and forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات. تمسَّكَ أصحابنا بما عُمْدَتُه الآيات والأحاديث الداله على أنَّ المؤمنين يدخلون الجنَّة البتّة ، كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] . وقوله : «مَن قال لا إله إلَّا الله دخل الجنَّة» وليس ذلك قبل دخول النَّار؛ فتعيَّن أنْ يكونَ بعده ، وهو مسألة انقطاع العذاب، أو بدونه وهو مسألة العفو التام.
The section on the audible matters (al-sam'iyyat). Our companions adhered to what are based on the verses and hadiths indicating that the believers enter Paradise definitely, such as His statement, the Most High: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [al-Zalzala:7]; and His statement: 'Whoever says there is no god but Allah enters Paradise.' And that is not before entering the Fire; so it is determined that it is after it, and it is the issue of the cessation of punishment, or without it, and it is the issue of complete forgiveness.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage is an introductory definition and proof for the section on Sam'iyyat. The author states that the Sunni scholars base their position on Quranic verses and hadiths that unequivocally indicate that believers will enter Paradise. He cites two proofs: the Quranic verse from al-Zalzala that even an atom's weight of good will be seen (and rewarded), and the famous hadith that whoever sincerely declares faith will enter Paradise. The author then makes a logical deduction: since this entry into Paradise is not before entering the Fire (i.e., it is not preceded by punishment by necessity, because some believers may enter directly), it is determined that the entry occurs either after a period of punishment (the issue of cessation of punishment) or without any punishment at all (the issue of complete forgiveness). Thus, the passage sets out the two possible outcomes for sinful believers: they may be punished and then admitted to Paradise, or they may be forgiven outright. This frames the subsequent discussion on whether some sinners must necessarily be punished.

Key Terms
السمعيات
الوعيد
العفو التام
انقطاع العذاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage implies that all believers will be punished before entering Paradise.
Correction: The passage actually states that believers will either be punished and then granted Paradise, or they will be forgiven outright without punishment. It does not assert that all believers must be punished.

Checkpoint

A) All believers enter Paradise before entering the Fire.
B) The entry into Paradise is not before the Fire; it is either after punishment or without punishment.
C) No believer enters the Fire at all.
D) Only those who committed major sins enter the Fire before Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The scriptural evidence guarantees Paradise for believers, but the timing—whether after punishment or without—is the central question of the Sam'iyyat section.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Necessity of the Divine Threat for a Group of Disobedient Muslims
The author explains the conditions and necessity of the threat being executed on some sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine regarding the divine threat of punishment for major sins: that it necessarily applies to a group of sinners, not every individual, and that the rest are subject to Allah's will.

Core Idea
The divine threat is certain for some, not all; the rest rest on Allah's will.

This passage from the Sanusi tradition explains a crucial point in Sunni theology regarding God's threat of punishment for major sins. It clarifies that although the threat is real and must be carried out, it applies to a group of sinners — not every individual — allowing for forgiveness and repentance. The author also addresses the status of minor sins and the inclusion of disbelievers, grounding the argument in the principle that God's speech is truthful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the careful qualifiers like 'ghayr mu'ayyan' (unspecified).
2
Identify the core doctrine
Locate the statement that the threat penetrates in a group (ta'ifa) and that the committer of a major sin does not abide eternally. This is the main thesis.
3
Understand the conditions
The passage lists conditions: the identity is unspecified because the specified individual may be forgiven; minor sins are excluded; disbelievers are included if 'nation of invitation' is meant. Note how each condition is justified.
4
Grasp the proof and conclusion
The proof is that Allah's speech is true, so the threat must be realized. The conclusion is that at least one from each sin category must be punished, and the rest are under Allah's will.
This affirms both God's justice and mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نُفوذ الوعيد في طائفة وعدم خلود مرتكب الكبيرة سمعاً وإجماعا . وقولنا: غيرَ مُعيَّن لأنَّ المُعيَّن يجوز العفو عنه (۱) مطلقا (۲) ، أو توفيقه للتّوبة ، وخرج بقولنا: من غير تأويل يُعذر به الصَّغير (۳) ؛ لغُفرانها باجتناب الكبائر، وجواز العفو عنها وإن لم يجتنب الكبائر، ودخل في البعض : الكَافِرُ (٤) ، بناءً على أنَّ المُراد أُمَّة الدَّعوة (ه) ؛ لأنَّهم مُكلَّفون بالفروع ، فلا بُدَّ مِن نُفوذ الوعيد في طائفة من العصاة ؛ لأنَّه تعالى توعدهم، وكلامه صدْقٌ (٦) ، والظَّاهر أنَّ المُراد (۷) : طائفةٌ مِن كلِّ صِنفٍ منهم (۸) ، لأَنَّ الله تعالى توعد كلَّ صِنفٍ على حِدَته ، وما سوى تلك الطَّائفة .. فحكمُه : أَنَّه في المشيئة (۱) قوله: (يجوز العفو عنه ) أي : عقلا وشرعا الشنواني . (۲) قوله: (مطلقا) أي: بتوبة وبدونها، وفي نسخة: حذف «مطلقا» وهي أولى، لأن موضوع المسألة أنه فعل الذنب ولم يتب منه . الشنواني. (ه) قوله : ( بناء على أنَّ المراد أمة الدعوة والمعتمدُ أنَّ المراد : أُمة الإجابة ؛ فلا يدخل الكافر، وعليه فلا يجوز طلب مغفرة جميع ذنوب كل المسلمين. الشنواني . (٦) قوله : ( وكلامه الصدق يقال هو على المشيئة ، نعم هو ظاهر قول الماتريدية بالتخصيص، والأولى الاستدلال بما ورد من تعذيب بعض الموحدين، فليتأمل؛ فقد لا يعم الأمير. الأنواع. (۷) قوله : (أن المراد بالبعض المعذب الشنواني. (۸) قوله: (من كل صنف منهم من العصاة كالزنا والسرقة والغصب، لأن المراد البعض من جميع العصاة. الشنواني. قسم السمعيات عند أهل السُّنَّة، وهكذا في كُلِّ صِنفٍ مِن العُصاة بصنف (۱) مِن الكبائر ، منهم أقلّها طائفة كالرُّنَاة والغُصَّاب وقَتَلَة الأَنْفُس ، لا بُدَّ مِن نُفوذ الوعيد في واحدٌ (٢) .
The penetration of the threat in a group and the non-eternity of the committer of a major sin, by hearing and consensus. And our saying: unspecified, because the specified one may be forgiven absolutely or granted repentance. And there exits by our saying: without an excuseable interpretation the minor sin, because it is forgiven by avoiding major sins, and the permissibility of forgiveness even if one does not avoid major sins. And enters in 'some' the disbeliever, based on the intended meaning being the nation of invitation, because they are obligated by the branches. So there must be penetration of the threat in a group of the disobedient, because He, exalted, threatened them, and His speech is truth. And the apparent meaning is that the intended is a group from every category of them, because Allah threatened each category separately, and what other than that group, its ruling is that it is under the will. (Then the footnote comments follow.) The section of the heard things. According to Ahl al-Sunna, and likewise in every category of the disobedient with a category of major sins, from them the least of it is a group like the fornicators, the usurpers, and murderers of souls, there must be penetration of the threat in one.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage defines and argues for the necessity of the divine threat (al-wa'id) being executed on some disobedient Muslims. It begins by stating the doctrine: the threat of punishment for major sins will be carried out on a group (ta'ifa) of sinners, not on all, and the sinner will not abide eternally in hell. The author then clarifies that the identity of the punished is not specified ('ghayr mu'ayyan') because the specified individual may be forgiven or granted repentance. This excludes minor sins, which are forgiven by avoiding major sins. The disbeliever is included in 'some' if the addressed nation is the ummat al-da'wa (those called to Islam), since they are obligated by the branches of the law. The proof: Allah threatened them, and His word is true, so the threat must be realized. The apparent meaning is that from each category of sin (e.g., fornication, theft) at least one person will be punished, because Allah threatened each category individually. As for those beyond that group, their matter is under Allah's will (mashi'a) according to Ahl al-Sunna. The passage then reiterates that in every category of sinners, at least one individual (even one) must suffer the threat, as the term 'ta'ifa' can apply to one by language.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the punishment is necessarily applied to a group of sinners, not to every individual, and that individuals may be forgiven or granted repentance.

The Fate of Sinful Believers and Refutation of Mu'tazila
The author proves the non-eternity of sinful believers and refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine of ihbat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theologians hold that no believer abides eternally in hell, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine of annulment of good deeds.

Core Idea
Faith ensures a believer's eventual salvation, even if punished temporarily.

This passage tackles a critical question in Islamic theology: what happens to a believer who commits major sins? The author presents a rational and scriptural argument to show that such a believer, even if punished in hell, will ultimately be saved because of their faith. He also uses this argument to refute the Mu'tazila claim that evil deeds can completely nullify good deeds, a doctrine known as ihbat.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text once, noting the key terms and flow. Then read the literal translation.
The passage uses several Qur'anic verses as evidence.
2
Identify the main claim about sinful believers
Notice that the author states we do not say a sinful believer abides eternally in hell. Look for the proof verses.
The author uses az-Zalzala 7 and al-Hijr 48.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the reasoning: faith is a good deed -> the believer must see its reward -> cannot see it before entering hell because then he would be brought out (contradicting 15:48) -> therefore the reward is seen after exit or through forgiveness.
This shows that the exit is not obligatory on Allah but by His promise.
4
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
The author points out that from his earlier statement about evil deeds being requited like for like, the Mu'tazila doctrine of ihbat is invalid. Consider why.
The Mu'tazila believed that major sins annul good deeds; here, evil deeds are punished but good deeds (faith) are rewarded separately.
5
Summarize the categories of mukallaf
The passage ends with a classification: disbelievers are eternally in fire, hypocrites are in the lowest depth, and believers are not eternal. Confirm this distinction.
This classification is based on the preceding arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ثُمَّ) مَن أرادَ الله تعذيبه مِن عُصاة المؤمنينَ لا نَقولُ بخلوده في (۲). النَّار، بل (الخُلُودُ مُجْتَنَبْ) أي اعتقاده (۳) ، فلا نأخذ به، لمثل قوله تعالى: يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ الله :[ الزلزلة : ۷] والإيمان عمل خير للعاصي ؛ فلا بُدَّ أنْ يَرى المؤمنُ جَزاءَهُ، ولا جائز أنْ يراه قبل دخول النار ثم يدخلها ؛ لقوله: ﴿وَمَا هُم مِّنْهَا بِمُخْرَجِينَ ﴾ [الحجر: ٤٨] . فتعيَّن أنه بعد الخروج منها إن قُدِّرَ له دخولها ، أو بعد العفو إن لم يُقَدَّر ذلك، وخروجه من النار ليس بطريق الوجوب عليه تعالى، بل بمقتضى ما سبق من الوعد ، كقوله تعالى: فَمَن زُحْزِحَ عَنِ النَّارِ وَأُدْخِلَ الْجَنَّةَ فَقَدْ فَازَ (٤) [آل عمران: ١٨٥] . وقد عُلم من قول المُصنف رحمه الله تعالى آنفا: «فالسيئات عنده بالمثل إلى هنا .. بطلان مذهب المعتزلة القائلين بإحباط السيّئاتِ الحسنات. كما علم منه (٥) أيضًا أَنَّ المُكلَّف إما : كافر فهو مُخلَّدٌ في النَّار ، ويختصُّ المنافق بالدرك الأسفل منها.
Then, whoever Allah wills to punish from the disobedient believers, we do not say he abides eternally in the fire, rather eternity is avoided, i.e., its belief, so we do not adopt it. For example of His statement: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (az-Zalzala 7) and faith is a good deed for the disobedient, so the believer must see his recompense, and it is not permissible that he sees it before entering the fire then enters it, because of His statement: 'And they will not be brought out of it' (al-Hijr 48). So it is determined that it is after exiting from it if entrance into it is decreed for him, or after forgiveness if that is not decreed. And his exit from the fire is not by way of obligation upon Him, exalted, but by virtue of what preceded of the promise, like His statement: 'Whoever is removed from the fire and admitted to paradise has indeed succeeded' (Aal 'Imran 185). And it is known from the author's statement earlier: 'The evil deeds with Him are like for like up to here...' the invalidity of the Mu'tazila school who claim the annulment of good deeds by evil deeds. And it is also known from it that the responsible is either: a disbeliever, so he is eternally in the fire, and the hypocrite is distinguished by the lowest depth of it.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage continues the argument on the fate of sinful Muslims. It states that even if Allah wills to punish a disobedient believer, we do not affirm eternal abiding (khulud) in hell for him. Rather, eternal abiding is 'avoided' (mujtanab) in belief. The proof is Qur'anic: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (99:7). Since faith is a good deed, the believer must see its reward. But if he were to see it before entering hell and then enter it, that would contradict the verse that they will not be brought out (15:48). Therefore, the seeing of reward must occur after exiting hell (if he enters it) or after forgiveness. His exit is not due to obligation on Allah but by His prior promise. The passage then notes that from the author's earlier statement about evil deeds being requited like for like, the Mu'tazila doctrine of annulment (ihbat) of good deeds by evil deeds is refuted. Finally, it states that the mukallaf is either a disbeliever (eternal in fire) or a believer (not eternal), and the hypocrite is in the lowest depth of hell.

Key Terms
الخلود
الوعيد
الإحباط
المنافق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any punishment in hell implies eternal damnation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that believers may be punished temporarily but will ultimately be saved because of their faith. Disbelievers face eternal punishment.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why must the sinful believer ultimately be saved from hell?
A) Because Allah is obliged to forgive all sins.
B) Because the believer's faith is a good deed that must be rewarded, and the verses about eternal exit only apply after exit.
C) Because the Mu'tazila doctrine of annulment is correct.
D) Because the believer will repent before death.

Key Takeaway
The faith of a believer ensures that any punishment in hell is temporary, and evil deeds do not annul the reward of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted the sincerity of your faith because of your sins, thinking that your good deeds are worthless? How does this passage's assertion that faith itself is a good deed that will be seen by Allah change your perspective?
?
If you knew that even the most sinful believer will eventually be saved, how does that affect your urgency to correct others or to fear Allah's punishment? Does it make you more hopeful or less careful?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that justice required evil deeds to annul good deeds. Do you ever treat your own or others' good deeds as canceled by mistakes? How can you hold onto the promise that faith remains even when you fall short?

The Sunni Position on Divine Threat and Eternal Punishment
Sanusi explains the Sunni view on divine threat and the non-eternity of punishment for sinful believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theology teaches that no believer will remain in Hell forever, based on the Qur'anic promise and the principle of divine justice.

Core Idea
Hell is not eternal for any believer; faith ensures eventual paradise.

This passage addresses a pivotal question in Islamic theology: the fate of believers who commit major sins. Unlike the Kharijites and Mu'tazilites, Sunnis affirm that faith itself secures an eventual exit from Hell. Here, the author establishes the scriptural and logical basis for this position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between divine threat (wa'id) and promise (wa'd).
The passage uses Qur'anic verses as proofs.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify that the passage asserts (1) the inevitability of executing the threat on at least one sinner per category, and (2) that no believing sinner will be eternally punished.
3
Analyze the proof from Qur'an 99:7
The verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' is used to argue that faith, being good, must be rewarded. This reward cannot be seen before entering Hell, so the sinner must exit Hell or be pardoned.
This is a key logical argument.
4
Understand the distinction between obligation and promise
The passage clarifies that God's mercy in removing the sinner from Hell is not an obligation but a fulfillment of His promise.
This avoids anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات عند أهل السُّنَّة، وهكذا في كُلِّ صِنفٍ مِن العُصاة بصنف مِن الكبائر، منهم طائفة كالزُّنَاة والغُصَّاب وقَتَلَة الأَنْفُس، لا بُدَّ مِن نُفوذ الوعيد في واحد. (ثُمَّ) مَن أرادَ الله تعذيبه مِن عُصاة المؤمنينَ لا نَقولُ بخلوده في النَّار، بل (الخُلُودُ مُجْتَنَبْ) أي اعتقاده، فلا نأخذ به، لمثل قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ والإيمان عمل خير للعاصي؛ فلا بُدَّ أنْ يَرى المؤمنُ جَزاءَهُ، ولا جائز أنْ يراه قبل دخول النار ثم يدخلها؛ لقوله: ﴿وَمَا هُم مِّنْهَا بِمُخْرَجِينَ﴾. فتعيَّن أنه بعد الخروج منها إن قُدِّرَ له دخولها، أو بعد العفو إن لم يُقَدَّر ذلك، وخروجه من النار ليس بطريق الوجوب عليه تعالى، بل بمقتضى ما سبق من الوعد، كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن زُحْزِحَ عَنِ النَّارِ وَأُدْخِلَ الْجَنَّةَ فَقَدْ فَازَ﴾. وقد عُلم من قول المُصنف رحمه الله تعالى آنفا: «فالسيئات عنده بالمثل إلى هنا» بطلان مذهب المعتزلة القائلين بإحباط السيّئاتِ الحسنات. كما علم منه أيضًا أَنَّ المُكلَّف إما كافر فهو مُخلَّدٌ في النَّار، ويختصُّ المنافق بالدرك الأسفل منها. وإمَّا مؤمن لم يُذنب قَطَّ كالأنبياء، فهو مُخلَّدٌ فى الجَنَّة إجماعاً. وإمَّا مؤمنٌ مُذنب تابَ مِن جريمته، فهو في الجنة قطعاً أو ظن. وإمَّا مؤمنٌ مُذنِبٌ لم يتب، والذَّنب صغيرة، فهو في المشيئة. وإمَّا مؤمنٌ مذنب لم يتب، والذَّنبُ كبيرة من الكبائر، فهو محلُّ النزاع، والصَّواب أنَّ حُكم الفاسق مِن المؤمنينَ الخُلود في الجنَّة، إمَّا ابتداءً بموجب العفو أو الشَّفاعة، وإمَّا بعد التّعذيب بالنَّار بقدر الذَّنب، والله أعلم.
The section of sam'iyyat according to the people of the Sunnah. And likewise, for every type of sinner with a type of major sin, there is a group like the adulterers, the usurpers, and the murderers of souls; the execution of the threat upon one of them is inevitable. Then, whoever God wills to punish from among the sinful believers, we do not say that he remains eternally in the Fire. Rather, 'eternal punishment is avoided' — that is, its belief is avoided, so we do not adopt it. For example, His statement, the Most High: 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [Zalzala 7] and faith is a good deed for the sinner; so the believer must see his recompense, and it is not permissible that he see it before entering the Fire and then enter it, because of His statement: 'And they will not be removed from it' [Hijr 48]. So it is determined that it is after exiting from it, if his entering it is decreed, or after pardon if that is not decreed. And his exit from the Fire is not by way of obligation upon Him, the Most High, but by virtue of what preceded of the promise, as in His statement, the Most High: 'Then whoever is removed from the Fire and admitted to Paradise has triumphed' [Aal 'Imran 185]. And it is known from the statement of the author, may God have mercy on him, earlier: 'So evil deeds with Him are meted out accordingly up to here' — the invalidity of the doctrine of the Mu'tazila who claim that good deeds cancel out evil deeds. And it is also known from it that the morally responsible person is either: a disbeliever — he is eternally in the Fire, and the hypocrite is distinguished with the lowest depth thereof. Or a believer who never committed a sin, like the prophets — he is eternally in Paradise by consensus. Or a believing sinner who repented from his crime — he is in Paradise definitively or presumably. Or a believing sinner who did not repent, and the sin is minor — he is under the will [of God]. Or a believing sinner who did not repent, and the sin is a major sin from the major sins — he is the subject of dispute. And the correct view is that the judgment of the fasiq from among the believers is eternal abode in Paradise, either initially by virtue of pardon or intercession, or after punishment in the Fire according to the extent of the sin. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument regarding the fate of believers who commit sins. It begins by affirming the Sunni position that for certain types of major sinners (adulterers, usurpers, murderers), divine threat (al-wa'id) must be executed on at least one person, though not necessarily on every individual sinner. The author then addresses the fate of sinful believers: they are not eternally punished in Hell. The term 'al-khulud mujtanab' (eternity is avoided) means we do not adopt the belief that any sinning believer will remain forever in Hell. Proof is given from the Qur'an: the verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (Zalzala 7) indicates that the believer will see the reward for his faith, which is a good deed. If he were to enter Hell forever, he would not see that reward after Hell. So the reward must be seen either after pardon (if he never enters Hell) or after exiting Hell (if he enters for a time). The exit from Hell is not obligatory on God but occurs by His promise. The passage then refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of 'ihbat' (cancellation of good deeds by evil deeds). The classification of the mukallaf (morally responsible person) follows: (1) a disbeliever (including hypocrites) is eternally in Hell; (2) a sinless believer (like prophets) is eternally in Paradise by consensus; (3) a repenting believer is in Paradise; (4) a believer with unrepented minor sins is under God's will; (5) a believer with unrepented major sins is the subject of dispute. The correct Sunni view is that such a fasiq will eventually enter Paradise, either by pardon/intercession or after punishment proportionate to the sin.

Key Terms
السمعيات (sam'iyyat)
الوعيد (al-wa'id)
الخلود (al-khulud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God's threat of punishment is unconditional and that any sinner, even a believer, will be eternally punished in Hell.
Correction: The Sunni position distinguishes between threat and promise: the threat may be executed on some, but the promise of reward for faith guarantees that no believer will remain eternally in Hell.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't a sinful believer be eternally punished in Hell?
A) Because God's mercy overrides His justice.
B) Because his faith is a good deed that must be rewarded, and eternal punishment would prevent that.
C) Because all sins are forgiven through repentance.
D) Because the threat of punishment is only for disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position asserts that sinful believers will not remain in Hell eternally, based on the promise of reward for the good of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your faith, however weak, will eventually save you from eternal punishment? Are you living as if that is certain, or are you presumptuous? What specific sin do you need to repent from today to align your life with that hope?
?
The passage teaches that God's punishment for believers is not eternal. Does this make you complacent about minor sins? How does knowing that you will see the reward of your faith change how you approach your daily struggles?
?
If you were to die tonight, would you be confident that your faith outweighs your sins? What is one change you can make starting today to ensure that the good deed of iman is manifest in your actions?

Classifying the Fate of Humanity: Sunnis on the Unrepentant Sinner
Sanusi classifies five types of mukallaf and presents the Sunni position on the unrepentant major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of people regarding their final abode and the Sunni ruling that the unrepentant major sinner (fasiq) will eventually enter Paradise.

Core Idea
Every believer, even the unrepentant major sinner, will ultimately abide in Paradise.

After establishing that no believer suffers eternal punishment, the author presents a comprehensive classification of all morally responsible beings. This taxonomy clarifies the fate of every category: disbelievers, prophets, repentant sinners, those with minor sins, and the controversial case of the unrepentant major sinner. The passage also refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of ihbat (that major sin nullifies good deeds).

Guided Path
1
Read the classification list
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the five categories of mukallaf that the author lists: disbeliever, sinless believer, repentant sinner, believer with minor sin, believer with major sin.
Each has a distinct ruling.
2
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazilite ihbat
The passage begins by noting that the earlier statement 'evil deeds are meted out accordingly' refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that major sins cancel all good deeds.
3
Focus on the fasiq category
The category of 'believer with unrepented major sin' (al-fasiq) is the subject of dispute. Note that the correct Sunni view is that they will eventually enter Paradise, either by pardon/intercession or after punishment.
This is the key takeaway.
4
Reflect on the role of intercession and pardon
The author mentions 'pardon or intercession' as means by which the fasiq may enter Paradise immediately, or punishment proportionate to the sin.
Intercession (shafa'a) is a Sunni doctrine.

The Life and Sustenance of Martyrs
The author states the necessity of believing that war martyrs are alive and provided with delights in Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that war martyrs are physically alive in the Barzakh and receive provision from Paradise, and that this belief is obligatory in Sunni doctrine.

Core Idea
Martyrs live a bodily life with provision in the intermediate realm.

This lesson examines a core belief of Ahl al-Sunnah regarding the martyrs of war. The author instructs us to affirm that the martyr’s physical body (haykal) is alive in the intermediate realm and that he is provided with delights from Paradise. We will unpack the meaning and necessity of this belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the direct command 'describe' and the term 'haykal'.
The passage is a concise doctrinal statement.
2
Understand 'haykal'
The word 'haykal' refers to the physical structure of the martyr—body and soul together. This indicates a bodily life, not merely spiritual.
This distinguishes the martyr's state from that of ordinary believers.
3
Grasp the obligation (wujub)
The commentary says 'believe necessarily'—affirming this life and provision is obligatory. Denial would contradict revealed texts.
This makes it part of essential Sunni creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حياة الشهداء وَصِف شَهِيدَ الحرب بالحياةِ وَرَزْقِهِ مِنْ مُشْتَهى الجِنَّاتِ وَصِفْ شَهِيدَ الحَرْبِ أي اعتقد وجوبًا اتصافَ هَيْكَلِ شَهِيدِ الحَرب
The life of martyrs. And describe the martyr of war as having life and his provision from the desired gardens. And describe the martyr of war, i.e., believe necessarily that the structure (haykal) of the war martyr is characterized
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement about the life of martyrs in the Barzakh (intermediate realm). It begins with the heading 'The Life of Martyrs' (hayat al-shuhada'), then quotes the verse (matn) from the poem: 'Describe the war martyr as having life and his provision from the desired gardens.' The commentary explains that this means one must hold as a necessary belief that the physical person (haykal, i.e., the composite of body and soul) of the war martyr is described with life and provision from Paradise. The word 'haykal' emphasizes that it is a bodily life, not merely spiritual. The belief is obligatory (wujub), meaning denial of this is impermissible.

Key Terms
شهيد الحرب
الحياة
الرزق
هيكل
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the life of martyrs is only spiritual or metaphorical, not bodily.
Correction: The text explicitly affirms a physical life in the Barzakh with provision, using the term 'haykal' to denote the bodily structure.

Checkpoint
What does the author say must be believed about the war martyr?
A) His soul is alive but not his body.
B) His structure (haykal) is alive and receives provision from Paradise.
C) He is dead and his reward is only in the afterlife.
D) He is alive but does not receive any provision.

Key Takeaway
War martyrs are physically alive in the Barzakh and receive provision from Paradise, a belief that is obligatory upon every Muslim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If a martyr's physical body receives provision from Paradise, how should that affect your view of your own body and its purpose in this life?

The Real Life of Battlefield Martyrs
Sanusi explains the Quranic verse on martyrs' life and the nature of their existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine that martyrs live a real, complete life involving both body and soul, and receive physical delights as provision from Allah.

Core Idea
Martyrs are truly alive with full bodily and spiritual perception.

The Quran explicitly rejects the notion that martyrs are dead. Instead, they are alive and provided for. This lesson unpacks the nuanced theological understanding of that life, affirming its reality, its physical dimension, and the honor it entails. We will explore the key evidence and the scholars' commentary to grasp the perfect nature of martyrs' existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse
Start by reading the verse from Surah Al Imran (3:169) and its implication that martyrs are not dead but alive with their Lord.
The verse is the primary proof for this doctrine.
2
Understand 'life' as real
Note that the passage asserts the life of martyrs is 'haqiqa' (real), not metaphorical. This is based on the apparent meaning of the Quran.
This counters the view that martyrs are only spiritually alive.
3
Grasp the perfection of their life
The commentary explains that their life is 'kamila' (perfect), involving both soul and body together, with complete perceptions like knowledge, hearing, and sight.
This distinguishes them from other deceased whose life is only spiritual.
4
Consider the provisions
Martyrs experience physical enjoyments such as eating, drinking, and clothing from Paradise, as part of their reward.
These are for delight, not need.
5
Submit to the unknown
The passage concludes that the exact quality of this life is known only to Allah, and believers must accept it with submission.
This reinforces the theme of tafwid (entrusting knowledge to Allah).

The Life of Martyrs
And describe the battlefield martyr as having life and his provision from the delights of gardens. (And describe the battlefield martyr, i.e. believe necessarily that the structure of the battlefield martyr is characterized with perfect life; because of the saying of the Exalted: 'And do not think that those who were killed in the path of God are dead; rather, they are alive with their Lord, receiving provision' [3:169]. And their life is real, due to the literal apparent meaning of the verse, and that they are provided with what they desire as the living are provided with eating, drinking, and clothing and other things. Al-Jazuli said: Their life is not the same as what is attached (to perfect life), i.e. the complete perceptions such as knowledge, hearing, and sight, and it is with both soul and body together, not the imperfect one attached only to the soul. So their life is complete even if its quality is unknown to us. And the dead, although all of them are alive and perceptions are established for them, the martyrs have a more complete life than others, and the prophets have a more complete life than the martyrs, because they attained the station of martyrs and exceeded them, because they are martyrs of love, which is higher than the martyrdom of swords. Their eating and drinking is for enjoyment, not for need. And al-Ramli mentioned that they pray in their graves, perform pilgrimage, and marry, and are rewarded for their deeds as honor, not obligation, because obligation ended with death. Some did not restrict marriage to their wives, so it may include houris and others—al-Shanwani. He said: (al-kamila) meaning its perfection is its attachment to both soul and body according to what God knows—al-Amir. (In a copy: and that their life) He said: (haqiqa) i.e. continuous and persistent in it, otherwise the return of the soul to the body in the grave is established for all dead, let alone martyrs, but it does not settle in them, and the fact that it is real does not require that the bodies be with it as they were in the world in terms of need for food, drink, and other observed bodily attributes, but they have another rule—al-Shanwani. He said: (and clothing) in a hidden manner known to the Lord; in short, the matter is one of submission and entrustment—al-Amir. He said: (said al-Jazuli) it is better to say 'and he said' with a conjunctive waw, because it is different from what preceded, not an explanation of it, so it is contrasted with what preceded that their life is real—al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage explains the doctrine of martyrs' life after death. It begins by quoting the verse from Surah Al Imran. The commentary clarifies that the life of martyrs is a perfect life involving both body and soul, in contrast to the life of other deceased which is only spiritual. Martyrs experience sensory pleasures as rewards. The commentary also compares martyrs to other believers and to prophets, noting that prophets have an even higher life. It mentions that martyrs can perform actions like prayer, pilgrimage, and marriage in the grave as a form of honor, not obligation. The passage ends with clarifying that the reality of this life is known only to God, and the believer must submit.

Believing in Martyrs' Life without Inquiry
al-Juzuli states the life of martyrs is unknowable and must be believed as revealed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation to believe in the life of martyrs without seeking to understand its modality, based on the principle of tafwid.

Core Idea
The life of martyrs is real but unknowable—our duty is to believe, not to inquire.

How can something be real yet beyond human comprehension? Al-Juzuli addresses this by laying down a fundamental principle for dealing with unseen realities: accept the revealed text without seeking to understand how. This passage teaches the proper attitude toward the life of martyrs, a matter of the unseen known only through revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and the English translation carefully. Notice the key terms: غير مكيفة (not conceptualized) and معقولة (comprehensible).
2
Identify the two obligations mentioned
Al-Juzuli states two obligations: belief in the life as revealed, and refraining from inquiry into its modality. Find them in the text.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the prohibition
Ask: Why can't we inquire? The passage gives two reasons: the life is not comprehensible to humans, and the only source of knowledge is reports (خبر), but no report clarifies the modality. Discuss the principle of tafwid.
4
Reflect on the broader application
Consider how this principle applies to other unseen matters (sam'iyyat). This is a key methodological rule in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الجزولي: وحياتهم غير مكيفة ولا معقولة للبشر؛ يجب الإيمان بها على ما جاء به ظاهر الشرع؛ ويجب الكف عن الخوض في كيفيتها؛ إذ لا طريق للعلم بها إلا من الخبر؛ ولم يرد فيها شيء يبين المراد.
Al-Juzuli said: And their life is not conceptualized and not comprehensible to humans; it is obligatory to believe in it according to what the apparent revealed law has brought; and it is obligatory to refrain from delving into its modality; for there is no way to know it except from reports; and nothing has come to clarify the intended meaning.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling about how to approach the life of martyrs. Al-Juzuli declares that the martyrs' life cannot be conceptualized or intellectually understood by humans. The correct response is to believe in it exactly as it has come in the revealed texts, without attempting to understand its modality. He justifies this by noting that the only source of knowledge about this life is transmitted reports, and since no explicit clarification of its nature has been given, one must avoid speculation. The passage emphasizes a principle of tafwid (consigning the modality to Allah) in matters of the unseen.

Key Terms

إيمان

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Al-Juzuli states it is not conceptualized or comprehensible, and we must refrain from inquiring into its modality.

Key Takeaway
Believing in the life of martyrs is obligatory, but understanding its modality is impossible and thus must be left alone.

The Life of Martyrs: Affirm Without Asking How
Defines the life of martyrs and states the ruling to believe without inquiring into modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the life of martyrs as a reality beyond human conception, the obligation to believe in it according to apparent revelation, and the definition of life as a quality entailing sensation, movement, and volition.

Core Idea
The life of martyrs is real but unknown in its modality — believe it, don't analyze it.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle topic from the samiyyat: the life of martyrs after death. Since this life is not like worldly life and cannot be grasped by human reason, the proper response is to affirm it exactly as revealed without attempting to understand its how. The passage also gives a general definition of life to clarify what is being affirmed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the three key imperatives: believe, refrain, and the reason.
Pay attention to the phrase 'غير مكيفة' (not qualified) — it sets the tone.
2
Identify the definition of life
The passage defines life (الحياة) as a quality that necessarily entails sensation, movement, and volition, or that allows knowledge. Consider how this applies to the martyrs.
The definition is general; the life of martyrs shares these attributes but its exact modality is unknown.
3
Reflect on why modality is unknown
The text says there is no way to know except through report (خبر), and no report clarifies it. Reflect on the wisdom of this — it preserves the mystery and tests faith.
This is a key principle in dealing with unseen matters (al-samiyyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حياة الشهداء غير مكيفة ولا معقولة للبشر؛ يجب الإيمان بها على ما جاء به ظاهر الشرع؛ ويجب الكف عن الخوض في كيفيتها؛ إذ لا طريق للعلم بها إلا من الخبر؛ ولم يرد فيها شيء يبين المراد؛ والحياة: كيفية يلزمها الحس والحركة والإرادية، أو تصح لمن قامت به العلم؛ جميعا.
The life of the martyrs is not qualified (ghayr mukayyafa) nor conceivable by humans; it is obligatory to believe in it according to the apparent meaning of the Law (zahir al-shar‘); and it is obligatory to refrain from delving into its modality; because there is no way to know it except through report (khabar); and nothing has come that clarifies the intended meaning. And life (al-hayah) is a quality (kayfiyya) that necessarily entails sensation (hiss), movement (haraka), and volition (iradiyya), or it is valid for one in whom knowledge subsists; all together.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the life of martyrs (shuhada'). It begins by stating that this life is not like worldly life: it is not qualified in a way familiar to us and is not conceivable by human reason. Therefore, one must believe in it as it has come in the apparent texts of the Qur'an and Sunnah, without attempting to understand its modality (kayfiyya). The reason is that the only path to knowledge of such unseen matters is through transmitted reports (khabar), and no report has detailed its nature. The passage then defines life in general (al-hayah) as a quality that entails sensation, movement, and volition; or alternatively, it is a quality that allows the possessor to have knowledge. The phrase 'all together' likely emphasizes that both definitions apply to the life of martyrs, though its exact modality remains unknown.

Correction: The text emphasizes that this life is 'not qualified' (غير مكيفة) and 'not conceivable' (لا معقولة), meaning it is unlike anything in this world and beyond human comprehension.

Key Takeaway
The life of martyrs is a real quality entailing sensation, movement, and volition, but its exact nature is known only through revelation and must be accepted without seeking how.

Definition of the Martyr of War
Definition with exceptions based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of the martyr of war and how sinful intentions affect the spiritual reward.

Core Idea
Pure intention is necessary for complete martyrdom; worldly rulings differ from spiritual rewards.

Sanusi begins by defining the martyr of war with precision, emphasizing the conditions of sincere intention and the absence of sin. He then extends the ruling to those killed in other righteous causes, but warns that mixed intentions can reduce the reward. This passage highlights the importance of sincerity in actions that appear outwardly praiseworthy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the careful wording and conditions. Do not analyze yet.
The passage is dense; let the author's own terms stand out.
2
Identify the key conditions for a perfect martyr
Look for the specific conditions: being a believer, killed in war against disbelievers, from a fighting cause, for exalting Allah's word, without any sinful cause.
3
Understand the extension to other causes
Note that the definition expands to anyone killed while fighting for truth—against rebels, highway robbers, or in commanding good—showing the principle extends beyond formal warfare.
This shows the comprehensiveness of the category.
4
Analyze the exception for sinful motives
Focus on the contrast for those who cheat in spoils or fight solely for material gain. They receive the worldly ruling of a martyr but not the full spiritual reward.
This distinction teaches that internal states matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بشهيد الحرب: المؤمن المقتول في حرب الكفار، بسبب من أسباب القتال، لإعلاء كلمة الله تعالى، بدون مقارفة سبب مؤثم؛ ومثله كل مقتول على الحق كالمجروح في قتال البغاة وقطاع الطريق وإقامة الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر؛ وأما المقتول في حرب الكفار لإعلاء كلمة الله تعالى لكن مع مقارفة سبب مؤثم كمن غل في الغنيمة أو محض القصد للغنيمة فله حكم شهداء الدنيا لا ثوابهم الكامل.
And what is meant by the martyr of war (shahid al-harb) is the believing person killed in a war against disbelief, due to a cause of fighting, to exalt the word of Allah, without committing a sinful cause; and likewise every person killed upon the truth, like one wounded in fighting against rebels (bughat) and highway robbers (qutta‘ al-tariq) and establishing the command of good and forbidding evil. As for one killed in a war against disbelief to exalt the word of Allah but with a sinful cause, like one who cheats in spoils (ghalla fi al-ghanima) or whose sole intention was the spoils, then he has the ruling of the martyrs of this world (shuhada’ al-dunya) but not their complete reward.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage defines the martyr of war (shahid al-harb) and then explains exceptions. It begins with a precise definition: a believing person killed in a war against disbelievers, from a cause directly related to the fighting, with the sincere intention of exalting Allah's word, and without any sinful cause (i.e., no treachery, stealing from spoils, etc.). Then it expands: anyone killed while fighting for truth—such as fighting rebels, highway robbers, or in commanding good and forbidding evil—has the same reward as a martyr of war. The passage then contrasts those who are killed in war against disbelievers with the stated intention but with a sinful cause, such as cheating in spoils (ghulul) or having solely material gain as motive. Such a person still receives the worldly ruling of a martyr (i.e., not washed, not prayed over) but does not receive the full otherworldly reward. This distinction emphasizes the importance of pure intention for complete martyrdom.

مقارفة سبب مؤثم
غل
الغنيمة
إعلاء كلمة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any death in battle makes one a perfect martyr with full reward.
Correction: The passage shows that sinful intentions, like stealing spoils or fighting for material gain, reduce the spiritual reward to only the worldly ruling, not the complete otherworldly reward.

Checkpoint

B) Ruling of a martyr in this world but no complete reward.
C) No reward at all.
D) He is not considered a martyr.

Key Takeaway
Pure intention is essential for the full reward of martyrdom; sinful motives reduce the otherworldly reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself doing something outwardly righteous—like helping someone or defending a truth—while inwardly your heart was attached to recognition, praise, or some material benefit? What specific act will you do today where you consciously work to purify your intention solely for Allah?

The text shows that even one killed in war for Allah can lose full reward if they cheated in spoils or fought only for spoils. Think of a good deed you regularly perform—prayer, charity, teaching. What concrete step can you take this week to strip away that attachment and make your intention purely for Allah?
?
When you die, you will not be able to control how others see you—your worldly 'ruling.' But Allah sees your inner intention. Are you more preoccupied with appearing righteous to others (like the worldly martyr) or with being genuinely sincere before Allah (like the complete martyr)? Three Categories of Martyrs
Classifies martyrs into three types based on worldly and otherworldly rewards.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of martyrs in Islamic law and how their worldly and otherworldly rewards differ.

Core Idea
Not all martyrs are equal — status depends on manner of death and intention.

This passage from al-Sanusi's text clarifies the classification of martyrs that goes beyond the common understanding of those killed in battle. It distinguishes three types based on worldly and otherworldly rewards, showing that even those who die from certain illnesses can attain martyrdom in the Hereafter, though they do not receive the same worldly rulings as war martyrs. Let's explore the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic and English to get a general sense. Pay attention to the specific terms for types of martyrs.
Notice that the passage begins by discussing those who die from stomach disease or plague.
2
Identify the three categories
As you read, locate the three types mentioned: martyr of both worlds, martyr of this world only, and martyr of the next world only. Notice the criteria that differentiate them.
3
Understand the rulings for each type
The text mentions specific rulings like washing and praying over the martyr of the next world only. Compare these to the war martyr who is not washed.
This shows the practical difference in worldly treatment.
4
Connect to the poet's statement
The author references the poet's wording to exclude the third type. Try to understand how that exclusion works by focusing on the description of the war martyr with life.
The poet's statement helps define the boundary of the war martyr category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المبطون والمطعون ونحوهما من شهداء الآخرة فقط؛ فإنه وإن كان كالأول في الثواب لكنه دونه في الحياة والرزق وأحكام الدنيا، فإنه يغسل ويصلى عليه؛ فظهر أن الشهداء ثلاثة: شهيد دنيا وآخرة، وشهيد دنيا فقط، وشهيد آخرة فقط، وهذا الثالث خرج بقول الناظم: وصف شهيد الحرب بالحياة بعد شموله للأولين، وإرادة الغنيمة أو الوقوع في المعصية لا ينافي حصول الشهادة.
As for one who dies of a stomach disease (mabtun) and one who dies of plague (mat‘un) and the like, they are martyrs of the next world only; for though he is like the first [i.e., the war martyr] in reward, he is below him in [the special] life and provision and the rulings of this world, for he is washed and prayed over. Thus it becomes clear that the martyrs are three: a martyr of this world and the next, a martyr of this world only, and a martyr of the next world only. And this third [type] is excluded by the statement of the poet: 'his description of the martyr of war with life' after it had included the first two, and intending spoils or falling into sin does not negate the attainment of martyrdom.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of martyrs into three categories. It begins by mentioning those who die from stomach disease (mabtun), plague (mat‘un), and similar ailments; these are martyrs of the next world only (shuhada’ al-akhira faqat). Their status: they receive the same reward in the Hereafter as the war martyr (the 'first' type), but they are inferior in terms of the special life (presumably the life of martyrs discussed earlier) and provision, and in this world they are washed and the funeral prayer is performed for them (unlike the war martyr who is not washed). From this, the author deduces three categories: 1) martyr of both worlds (shahid dunya wa-akhira) — the pure war martyr; 2) martyr of this world only (shahid dunya faqat) — the one killed with a sinful motive; 3) martyr of the next world only (shahid akhira faqat) — those who die from disease or similar. The third type is derived from the poet's (nazim) statement that described the war martyr with life (i.e., the special life), indicating that the war martyr has that life in this world, while those with disease do not. Finally, it notes that intending spoils or committing sin does not negate the attainment of martyrdom entirely (i.e., they still get some rank, but not the full status).

Key Terms
المبطون
المطعون
شهيد دنيا وآخرة
شهيد دنيا فقط
شهيد آخرة فقط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All martyrs receive identical status in worldly and otherworldly matters.
Correction: The passage shows that martyrs of the next world only are washed and prayed over, unlike war martyrs, and they are 'below' in life and provision, although alike in reward.

Checkpoint
Which type of martyr is washed and prayed over according to the passage?
A) Shahid dunya wa-akhira
B) Shahid dunya faqat
C) Shahid akhira faqat
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Martyrs are classified into three categories based on their manner of death and intention, with differing worldly and otherworldly statuses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die tonight, which of the three categories would your death fall under? What does that reveal about the true intentions behind your actions?
?
The text teaches that even a person who intended spoils in battle still attains some degree of martyrdom. Does this challenge your understanding of the relationship between your inner motives and your ultimate standing with Allah?
?
Consider the category of 'martyr of both worlds.' What concrete changes would you need to make in your life — in your intentions, actions, and fears — to sincerely hope for that rank?

The Three Categories of Martyrs (Shuhadāʾ)
Classification of martyrs into three categories with distinct rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of martyrs in Islamic law and how their worldly and afterlife rulings differ.

Core Idea
Martyrdom has categories: not all who die fighting are equal in reward or ruling.

In this lesson, we examine the commentator's classification of martyrs into three types based on their worldly status and afterlife reward. This distinction is crucial for understanding funeral rites and the nature of martyrdom. The passage clarifies that while all named 'martyrs' share honor, their rulings vary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three categories and the numbers indicating footnotes or commentary divisions.
The passage uses parenthetical numbers (1) to (8) which likely refer to the original poem's lines.
2
Identify the first category
Find the description of 'everyone killed in truth'—this includes fighting rebels, highway robbers, and those killed while enjoining good and forbidding evil. These are martyrs of both worlds.
3
Contrast with the second category
Look at the one killed in jihad against disbelievers but with a sinful motive (like betraying spoils or pure desire for booty). They have worldly martyr status but not complete reward.
This shows that intention affects afterlife reward.
4
Understand the third category
Examine those who die from abdominal disease, plague, or similar causes. They are martyrs of the Hereafter only—their worldly ruling includes washing and praying over them, unlike the first two.
This category is often overlooked.
5
Summarize and note the exception
The commentator explicitly states the three types: (1) martyr of both worlds, (2) martyr of this world only, (3) martyr of the Hereafter only. Note that the third is excepted from the poet's description of 'the war martyr with life'.
Also note that desiring booty or disobedience does not negate martyrdom entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات
كلُّ مقتول على الحقِّ كالمجروح في قتال البغاة وقُطَّاع الطريق (١) وإقامة الأمر بالمعروف (٢) والنهي عن المنكر ؛ وأمَّا المقتول في حرب الكُفَّار لإعلاء كلمة الله تعالى لكن مع مُقارفة سبب مُؤتِّم كمَن غلّ (٣) في الغنيمة أو مَحَضَ القصد للغنيمة (٤) فله حكم شُهداء الدُّنيا (٥) لا ثوابهم الكامل، وأما المبطون والمطعون ونحوهما (٦) مِن شهداء الآخرة فقط ؛ فإنَّه وإِنْ كانَ كالأَوَّل الثَّواب (٧) لكنَّه دونه في الحياة والرّزق وأحكام الدُّنيا، فإنَّه يُغسل ويُصلَّى عليه ؛ فظهر أنَّ الشُّهداء ثلاثة: شهيد دنيا وآخرة ، وشهيد دنيا فقط، وشهيد آخرة فقط ، وهذا الثَّالث خرج بقول النَّاظم : وصف شهيد الحرب بالحياة» بعد شموله للأوَّلَيْن (٨) ، وإرادة الغنيمة أو الوقوع في المعصية لا يُنافي حُصول الشهادة.
Section on samʿiyyāt (matters heard from revelation). Everyone killed in truth, like the wounded in fighting rebels and highway robbers (1) and in establishing enjoining good (2) and forbidding evil. As for the one killed in war against disbelievers for exalting the word of God Most High but while committing a sinful cause, like one who betrays (3) in the spoils or whose sole intent is booty (4), he has the ruling of the martyrs of this world (5), not their complete reward. As for the one who dies of abdominal disease, and one who dies of plague, and similar (6), they are only martyrs of the Hereafter. Indeed, though he is like the first in reward (7), he is inferior in life, provision, and worldly rulings, for he is washed and prayed over. So it becomes clear that the martyrs are three: a martyr of this world and the Hereafter, a martyr of this world only, and a martyr of the Hereafter only. This third one is excepted by the poet's saying: 'describing the war martyr with life' after its inclusion of the first two (8), and desiring booty or falling into disobedience does not negate the attainment of martyrdom.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a classification of martyrs (shuhadāʾ) into three categories based on their worldly and afterlife rulings. The commentator, building on the poet's lines, begins by defining the first category: 'everyone killed in truth' — those who die fighting for a just cause, such as against rebels (al-bughā) or highway robbers (quṭṭāʿ al-ṭarīq), or those killed while establishing the duty of enjoining good and forbidding evil. These are martyrs of both worlds (shahīd dunyā wa ākhira): in this world, they are not washed and not prayed over (implied by the contrast later), and in the Hereafter they receive full reward. The second category is the one killed in jihad against disbelievers but with a sinful motive, like betraying in spoils (ghulūl) or pure desire for booty. Such a person has the worldly ruling of a martyr — meaning he is not washed or prayed over — but he does not receive the complete reward of the Hereafter. The third category includes those who die from abdominal illness (mabṭūn), plague (maṭʿūn), and similar causes; these are 'martyrs of the Hereafter only'. Their ruling in this world is different: they are washed and prayed over, though their reward in the Hereafter is like that of the first category. The commentator then summarizes the three types explicitly: (1) martyr of both worlds, (2) martyr of this world only, (3) martyr of the Hereafter only. He notes that the third type is excluded from the poet's statement 'describing the war martyr with life' which covers the first two. Finally, he clarifies that intending booty or falling into disobedience does not prevent the attainment of martyrdom per se, though it may affect the reward.

Key Terms

شهيد دنيا وآخرة
شهيد دنيا فقط
شهيد آخرة فقط
سمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All those called 'martyrs' share the same burial ruling (not washed, not prayed over).
Correction: Only the first two categories (martyr of both worlds and martyr of this world only) are not washed or prayed over. The third category (martyr of the Hereafter only) is washed and prayed over.

Checkpoint
Which category of martyr is washed and prayed over (i.e., has the worldly ruling of an ordinary deceased person)?
A) The martyr of both this world and the Hereafter
B) The martyr of this world only
C) The martyr of the Hereafter only
D) All three categories

Key Takeaway
The three categories of martyrs differ in worldly rulings and reward: only the martyr of the Hereafter alone is washed and prayed over.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How can you protect yourself from falling into the category of 'this world only'? What practical step will you take today to purify your intention?
?
Do you treat those who are martyrs in the Hereafter but receive normal burial in this world with less honor? How does this lesson challenge your perception of who is truly successful? Will you change how you view those who die from illnesses versus those who die in battle?
